<p><b>Montana State University</b> (<b>MSU</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Land-grant_university" title="Land-grant university">land-grant university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Bozeman,_Montana" title="Bozeman, Montana">Bozeman, Montana</a>, United States. It is the state's largest university<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missoulian.com_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-missoulian.com-5">[5]</a></sup> and flagship campus of the Montana State University System, which is part of the <a href="/wiki/Montana_University_System" title="Montana University System">Montana University System</a>.  MSU offers baccalaureate degrees in 51 fields, master's degrees in 41 fields, and doctoral degrees in 18 fields through its nine colleges. The university regularly reports annual research expenditures in excess of $100 million, including a record $130.8 million in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>More than 16,900 students attend MSU,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-7">[7]</a></sup> and the university faculty numbers, including department heads, are 602 full-time and 460 part-time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>  The university's main campus in Bozeman is home to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KUSM" title="KUSM">KUSM</a> television, <a href="/wiki/KGLT" title="KGLT">KGLT</a> radio, and the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_the_Rockies" title="Museum of the Rockies">Museum of the Rockies</a>. MSU provides outreach services to citizens and communities statewide through its agricultural experiment station and 60 county and reservation extension offices.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_the_college">Establishment of the college</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Establishment of the college">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_N_at_Montana_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1931" data-file-width="3000" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Looking_N_at_Montana_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg/220px-Looking_N_at_Montana_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Looking_N_at_Montana_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg/330px-Looking_N_at_Montana_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Looking_N_at_Montana_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg/440px-Looking_N_at_Montana_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Looking_N_at_Montana_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Montana Hall, the second building constructed on campus. The Office of the President is located here.</div></div></div>
<p>Montana became a state on November 8, 1889. Several cities competed intensely to be the state capital, the city of Bozeman among them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bozemandailychronicle.com_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-bozemandailychronicle.com-9">[9]</a></sup> In time, the city of <a href="/wiki/Helena,_Montana" title="Helena, Montana">Helena</a> was named the state capital. As a consolation, the state legislature agreed to put the state's <a href="/wiki/Land-grant_university" title="Land-grant university">land-grant college</a> in Bozeman. <a href="/wiki/Gallatin_County,_Montana" title="Gallatin County, Montana">Gallatin County</a> donated half of its 160-acre poor farm for the campus, and money for an additional 40 acres, which had been planned to hold a state capital, was raised by the community, including a $1,500 donation from rancher and businessman <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Story" title="Nelson Story">Nelson Story, Sr.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> This land, as well as additional property and monetary contributions, was now turned over to the state for the new college.
</p><p>MSU was founded in 1893 as the <b>Agricultural College of the State of Montana</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>  It opened on February 16 with five male and three female students. The first classes were held in rooms in the county high school, and later that year in the shuttered Bozeman Academy (a private <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University-preparatory_school" title="University-preparatory school">preparatory school</a>). The first students were from Bozeman Academy, and were forced to transfer to the college. Only two faculty existed on opening day: Luther Foster, a horticulturalist from <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a> who was also Acting President, and Homer G. Phelps, who taught business. Within weeks, they were joined by S.M. Emery (who ran the agricultural experiment station) and Benjamin F. Maiden (an English teacher from the former Bozeman Academy). <a href="/wiki/Augustus_M._Ryon" title="Augustus M. Ryon">Augustus M. Ryon</a>, a coal mine owner, was named the first president of the college on April 17, 1893. Ryon immediately clashed with the board of trustees and faculty. Where the trustees wanted the college to focus on agriculture, Ryon pointed out that few of its students intended to go back to farming. While the rapidly expanding faculty wanted to establish a <a href="/wiki/Remedial_education" title="Remedial education">remedial education</a> program to assist unprepared undergraduates (Montana's elementary and secondary public education system was in dire shape at the time), Ryon refused. The donation of the Story land to the college occurred in 1894, but Ryon was forced out in 1895 and replaced by the Rev. Dr. <a href="/wiki/James_R._Reid" title="James R. Reid">James R. Reid</a>, a Presbyterian minister who had been president of the Montana College at <a href="/wiki/Deer_Lodge,_Montana" title="Deer Lodge, Montana">Deer Lodge</a> since 1890.
</p><p>The college grew quickly under Reid, who provided 10 years of stability and harmony. The student body grew so fast that the high school building was completely taken over by the college. A vacant store on Main Street was rented to provide additional classroom space. Both the Agricultural Experiment Station (now known as Taylor Hall) and the Main Building (now known as Montana Hall) were constructed in 1896, although the agricultural building was the first to open. Both structures were occupied in 1898. The university football team was established in 1897, and the college graduated its first four students that same year. The curriculum expanded into civil and electrical engineering in 1898.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_and_growth_under_Hamilton_and_Atkinson">Expansion and growth under Hamilton and Atkinson</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Expansion and growth under Hamilton and Atkinson">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1546" data-file-width="2050" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Hamilton_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/220px-Hamilton_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Hamilton_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/330px-Hamilton_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Hamilton_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/440px-Hamilton_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mission Revival-style Hamilton Hall, the first campus dormitory, constructed in 1910.</div></div></div>
<p>Reid resigned for health reasons in 1905, and was succeeded by Dr. <a href="/wiki/James_M._Hamilton" title="James M. Hamilton">James M. Hamilton</a>, an economist. Determined to make the college into a school of technology, he rapidly expanded the curriculum areas such as biology, chemistry, engineering, geology, and physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">[12]</a></sup> Hamilton also devised the university motto, "Education for Efficiency", which the college continued to use until the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bozemandailychronicle.com_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-bozemandailychronicle.com-9">[9]</a></sup> Further marking this change in direction, the school was officially renamed the <b>Montana College of Agriculture and Mechanic Arts</b> in 1913<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> (although that name was in widespread use as early as 1894). The college's first great rapid expansion of physical plant also began under Hamilton. Constructed during this time were Linfield Hall (1908), Hamilton Hall (1910), and Traphagen Hall (1919). The giant whitewashed "M" on the side of the Mount Baldy in the foothills of the Bridger Range was first built in 1916, and in 1917 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ROTC" title="ROTC">ROTC</a> came to campus for the first time.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Montana_State_University_Bozeman,_Romney_Gym.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Montana_State_University_Bozeman%2C_Romney_Gym.jpg/220px-Montana_State_University_Bozeman%2C_Romney_Gym.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Montana_State_University_Bozeman%2C_Romney_Gym.jpg/330px-Montana_State_University_Bozeman%2C_Romney_Gym.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Montana_State_University_Bozeman%2C_Romney_Gym.jpg/440px-Montana_State_University_Bozeman%2C_Romney_Gym.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Montana_State_University_Bozeman,_Romney_Gym.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iconic domed Romney Gym, home of the 1928 national champion Bobcat basketball team, was constructed in 1922.</div></div></div>
<p>Hamilton resigned in 1919 to become Dean of Men,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">[12]</a></sup> and his successor was agricultural expert <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Atkinson_(university_president)" title="Alfred Atkinson (university president)">Alfred Atkinson</a>. Atkinson's tenure lasted 17 years (1920 to 1937). A firm believer in Hamilton's vision for the school, Atkinson worked hard to continue the rapid expansion of the campus. The iconic, domed Gymnasium Building (now Romney Gym) was built in 1922, replacing a dilapidated "drill hall" and giving the school's men's basketball team its first home court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> The Heating Plant, Lewis Hall, and Roberts Hall followed in 1923. By the 1920s, the school was commonly referred to as <b>Montana State College</b> (<b>MSC</b>). Herrick Hall followed in 1926. The college was justifiably proud of its academic accomplishments, but its sports teams entered a golden age as well. In 1922, Atkinson hired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Ott_Romney" title="George Ott Romney">George Ott Romney</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schubert_Dyche" title="Schubert Dyche">Schubert Dyche</a> as co-head coaches of the football and men's basketball teams. Between 1922 and 1928 (the year he departed Montana for <a href="/wiki/Brigham_Young_University" title="Brigham Young University">Brigham Young University</a>), Romney's football teams compiled a 28–20–1 record. This included the 1924 season in which his team went undefeated until the final game of the year. As co-head basketball coach, Romney's teams compiled a 144–31 record and invented the <a href="/wiki/Fast_break" title="Fast break">fast break</a>. After Romney left, Schubert Dyche coached the "Golden Bobcats" team of 1928, which had a 36–2 record and won the national championship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In his seven years as basketball coach, Dyche's teams compiled a 110–93 record (this included the dismal 1932–33 and 1933–34 seasons), but won their conference championship twice. In 1930, the college built Gatton Field, a football field on what is now the site of the Marga Hosaeus Fitness Center. In one of President Atkinson's last accomplishments, the Dormitory Quadrangle (now Atkinson Quadrangle) was built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first three decades of the 20th century were rowdy ones on the college campus. Bozeman had a large <a href="/wiki/Red-light_district" title="Red-light district">red-light district</a> by 1900, alcohol was plentiful and cheap, and there was little in the way of organized entertainment such as theaters to occupy the student body. President Reid spent much of his presidency cracking down on dancing, drinking, gambling, and prostitution by students. President Hamilton sought to improve the atmosphere for women by building Hamilton Hall, which was not only the first on-campus housing for students but also the first all-women's housing on campus. Access by men to Hamilton Hall was strictly limited to young teenage boys (who acted as servants); adult males were permitted only in the first floor lounge, and only on Sundays. Atkinson Quadrangle was built on the location of the College Inn, also known as the "Bobcat Lair," a popular student drinking and dancing hangout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Depression_and_World_War_II">Depression and World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Depression and World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_SSW_at_Strand_Union_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1656" data-file-width="2500" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Looking_SSW_at_Strand_Union_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/220px-Looking_SSW_at_Strand_Union_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Looking_SSW_at_Strand_Union_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/330px-Looking_SSW_at_Strand_Union_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Looking_SSW_at_Strand_Union_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/440px-Looking_SSW_at_Strand_Union_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Looking_SSW_at_Strand_Union_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Student Union Building (now Strand Union Building), which opened in 1940.</div></div></div>
<p>The college suffered greatly during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression</a>. The price of agricultural products (Montana's economic mainstay) soared during World War I, as European and Russian farms were devastated by military campaigns and American and European armies demanded food. For a few years after the war, these prices stayed high. But as European agriculture got back on its feet, an agricultural depression swamped the United States beginning about 1923. State tax revenues plunged, and fewer buildings were constructed on campus after 1923. The United States entered the Great Depression in 1929. President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> established the <a href="/wiki/Public_Works_Administration" title="Public Works Administration">Public Works Administration</a> (PWA) in 1933 to provide federal funding for public works construction as a means of economic stimulus. But President Atkinson was strongly opposed to Roosevelt's <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>, and refused to accept PWA funds to expand the college. With the state unable to assist, Montana State College stagnated through the 1930s.
</p><p>President Atkinson resigned in 1937 to become president of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Arizona" title="University of Arizona">University of Arizona</a>. <a href="/wiki/A._L._Strand" title="A. L. Strand">A. L. Strand</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Entomology" title="Entomology">entomologist</a> who had discovered ways of controlling the devastating <a href="/wiki/Locust" title="Locust">locust</a> invasions in Montana, was named the new president. Strand was the first graduate of the college to become its president. An upsurge in campus drinking occurred after the end of <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">Prohibition</a>, and in 1940 the Student Union Building (now Strand Union Building) was built to provide students with a gathering spot on campus that (it was hoped) would keep them away from the saloons downtown.
</p><p>President Strand resigned his office in 1942 to accept the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Oregon_State_University" title="Oregon State University">Oregon State University</a>. (He remained there for 19 years, turning Oregon State into a world-class research and teaching institution.) With Montana still not yet having emerged from the Great Depression, the college struggled to find a new president. Engineering professor William Cobleigh took over as Acting President until from 1942 to 1943 while a replacement for Strand was found. During Cobleigh's year as president, college enrollment plunged as young men entered the armed forces or left to work in war industry plants on the West Coast. Nonetheless, federal funding increased as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War" title="United States Department of War">United States Department of War</a> sought rapid, significant increases in the number of chemical, engineering, and physics graduates to feed the war effort.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Renne_years">The Renne years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The Renne years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roland_renne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3899" data-file-width="2657" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Roland_renne.jpg/200px-Roland_renne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Roland_renne.jpg/300px-Roland_renne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Roland_renne.jpg/400px-Roland_renne.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roland_renne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dr. Roland Renne, president of MSU from 1943 to 1964.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1943, the state board of higher education appointed MSC economist Roland "Rollie" Renne to be the new acting president of the college. Renne was a protege of nationally known liberal economists <a href="/wiki/Richard_T._Ely" title="Richard T. Ely">Richard T. Ely</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_R._Commons" title="John R. Commons">John R. Commons</a> and a strong proponent of the New Deal. He'd taught at MSC since 1930, although he'd taken a leave of absence in 1942 to become the director of Montana's <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Price_Administration" title="Office of Price Administration">Office of Price Administration and Civilian Supply</a> (a federal wartime agency). Renne was named the permanent president of the college on July 1, 1944.
</p><p>Renne was president of the college for 21 years, the third-longest of any individual (as of 2013). With the passage of the <a href="/wiki/G.I._Bill" title="G.I. Bill">G.I. Bill</a> just eight days before his appointment and the end of the war in sight, Renne realized that servicemen returning from the war were going to flood college campuses. Renne quickly began hiring additional faculty and recycled wartime wooden buildings from around the state to build temporary classroom and housing space. His foresight helped the college survive the rapid rise in enrollment, which doubled from 1,155 in 1945 to 2,014 in 1946 and then nearly doubled again in 1947 to 3,591. Faculty numbers also skyrocketed, from 132 in 1945 to 257 in 1950. Believing that a college education was as much about instilling democratic values as teaching skills and trades, Renne rapidly changed the curriculum to emphasize liberal arts such as anthropology, archeology, history, political science, psychology, and sociology. Although the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Montana" title="University of Montana">University of Montana</a> (long considered the state's "liberal arts college", while MSC was the "ag school") opposed much expansion in this area, Renne successfully established a Department of Education, reconstituted the School of Business, and established new undergraduate and graduate programs in architecture, geography, geology, military science, and other disciplines.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_N_at_Brick_Breed_Fieldhouse_-_parking_lot_construction_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1117" data-file-width="3000" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Looking_N_at_Brick_Breed_Fieldhouse_-_parking_lot_construction_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/300px-Looking_N_at_Brick_Breed_Fieldhouse_-_parking_lot_construction_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Looking_N_at_Brick_Breed_Fieldhouse_-_parking_lot_construction_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/450px-Looking_N_at_Brick_Breed_Fieldhouse_-_parking_lot_construction_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Looking_N_at_Brick_Breed_Fieldhouse_-_parking_lot_construction_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/600px-Looking_N_at_Brick_Breed_Fieldhouse_-_parking_lot_construction_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Looking_N_at_Brick_Breed_Fieldhouse_-_parking_lot_construction_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Brick Breeden Fieldhouse and Worthington Arena, constructed in 1958.</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout the 1950s, Renne worked to rapidly expand the college's physical plant. During his presidency, 18 major buildings were constructed on campus — more than double the number that had been built between 1893 and 1944, and almost as many as were built between 1966 and 2013. These included the 1949 Library Building (now Renne Library), the campus' first dedicated library (it had previously been housed in a few rooms on the second floor of Montana Hall), and the 1958 <a href="/wiki/Brick_Breeden_Fieldhouse" title="Brick Breeden Fieldhouse">Brick Breeden Fieldhouse</a> (which supplemented the aging, outdated Romney Gym). The construction program included a chapel (Danforth Chapel in 1950), five large classroom buildings (McCall Hall in 1952, A.J.M. Johnson Hall in 1954, Reid Hall in 1959, Cooley Laboratory in 1960, and Gaines Hall in 1961), and seven residential and dining halls (Hannon Hall in 1954; Johnstone Hall in 1955; Culbertson Hall, Harrison Dining Hall, Mullan Hall, and Langford Hall in 1955; and Hapner Hall in 1959). Begun under his presidency but completed the year after he left were three more residential and dining halls (North Hedges, South Hedges, and Miller Dining Hall).
</p><p>There was some criticism that Renne did not pay full attention to the college in the 1950s. His governance style was somewhat authoritarian, and his extended absences led to leadership vacuums. He agreed to consulting roles with the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Water_Resources_Policy_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Water Resources Policy Commission (page does not exist)">Water Resources Policy Commission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutual_Security_Agency" title="Mutual Security Agency">Mutual Security Agency</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">United States Department of State</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Health,_Education_and_Welfare" title="United States Department of Health, Education and Welfare">United States Department of Health, Education and Welfare</a> throughout the 1950s that often took him away from campus for weeks at a time. He took a leave of absence from the college to become <a href="/wiki/Under_Secretary_of_Agriculture_for_Farm_and_Foreign_Agricultural_Services" title="Under Secretary of Agriculture for Farm and Foreign Agricultural Services">Assistant Secretary of Agriculture for International Affairs</a> from 1963 to 1964.
</p><p>Dr. Renne resigned as president of Montana State College effective January 1, 1964, to run for Governor of Montana. He lost the election, 51.4 to 48.6 percent, to incumbent governor <a href="/wiki/Tim_Babcock" title="Tim Babcock">Tim Babcock</a>.
</p><p>Campus life was not without its controversy during Renne's tenure, either. With <a href="/wiki/McCarthyism" title="McCarthyism">McCarthyism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communist</a> feeling running high in the country, Renne sought to protect the campus from political investigations by restricting student speech and assembly. He also restricted the kind of speakers who visited the campus, most famously denying former First Lady <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a> and literary critic <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Fiedler" title="Leslie Fiedler">Leslie Fiedler</a> the right to speak on campus. Other incidents also brought notoriety to campus. On March 7, 1957, 1,000 male students engaged in a "<a href="/wiki/Panty_raid" title="Panty raid">panty raid</a>" on Hannon Hall. It turned into a riot that took all night to control.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_status_and_campus_conservatism">University status and campus conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: University status and campus conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cobleigh_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="1882" height="292" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Cobleigh_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/220px-Cobleigh_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Cobleigh_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/330px-Cobleigh_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Cobleigh_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/440px-Cobleigh_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cobleigh_Hall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cobleigh Hall, completed in 1970 as an addition to Roberts Hall, houses several departments in the College of Engineering.</div></div></div>
<p>In February 1964, Dr. <a href="/wiki/Leon_H._Johnson" title="Leon H. Johnson">Leon H. Johnson</a> was appointed president of MSC. A research chemist who joined the college in 1943, he had most recently been the Executive Director of school's Endowed and Research Foundation (at the time, MSC's largest research unit) and Dean of the Graduate Division. Deeply committed to the college's research function, he pushed for MSC to be named a university — a change Renne had since the early 1950s, and which the Montana state legislature approved on July 1, 1965. At that time, the school received its new name, <b>Montana State University</b> (<b>MSU</b>). Bachelor's degree programs in economics, English, history, music, political science, and other disciplines were quickly established, as was the first university honors program. Johnson was a devoted admirer of the arts, and MSU's art and music programs blossomed. Johnson quickly worked to end the acrimonious relationship with the University of Montana, and the two schools began to present a united front to the state legislature.
</p><p>In 1966, Johnson altered and enlarged the university's administrative structure to help cope with increasing enrollment and increasing campus complexity. These changes included creating a 12-member executive council to advise him. The council included newly created vice presidents — overseeing areas such as academic affairs, administration, finance and research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Johnson was deeply conservative — fiscally, socially, and politically. He was deeply committed to continuing Renne's educational plan, but declined to spend money on new buildings (preferring to consolidate and renovate rather than expand). He also continued Renne's policies largely barring from campus speakers who were not clearly in the political mainstream. Johnson's policies were largely supported by the student body and the taxpaying public. MSU practiced a policy known as <i><a href="/wiki/In_loco_parentis" title="In loco parentis">in loco parentis</a></i>, in which it acted as a "parent" toward the "children" attending school there. Students themselves accepted these restrictions, which included dress codes, older adult chaperones at dances, a ban on alcohol, and mandatory military training for freshmen and sophomores. Although many American college campuses were engulfed by student radicalism, MSU's student body was as conservative as Johnson was, however, and for many years the biggest issues on campus were ending Saturday morning classes and building student parking lots.
</p><p>There were some campus protests, however. The first protest against the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> occurred in 1966 (drawing about 100 students), two underground student newspapers briefly appeared, and some students organized clubs to debate issues of the day. There were minor faculty and student protests when Johnson attempted to prevent English professor James Myers from assigning students to read <a href="/wiki/James_Baldwin" title="James Baldwin">James Baldwin</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Another_Country_(novel)" title="Another Country (novel)">Another Country</a></i>, and in the summer of 1968 a few faculty organized a symposium on the war. When about 150 students rallied in front of Montana Hall in 1969 to ask for co-ed and "open visitation" dorms (e.g., to allow men into women's dorm rooms, and vice versa), Johnson threatened to call out the city police.
</p><p>MSU's Bobcat Stadium saw its genesis during the Johnson years. Growing student unrest over the football team's use of decrepit Gatton Field (while the basketball team used modern Brick Breeden Fieldhouse) led to a proposal by Johnson in April 1968 to build a 16,000-seat stadium funded by student fees. The proposal failed in December 1968 after students argued that the university should concurrently build a new fitness center as well.
</p><p>President Johnson died of a heart attack on June 18, 1969. He'd suffered a heart attack in October 1968, and then underwent surgery out of state in April 1969.
</p><p>William Johnstone, a professor of education and Vice President for Administration at MSU, took over as Acting President. He was the first and (as of 2013) the only Montanan to become president of MSU. Johnstone pledged to build the fitness center first, and in December 1969 the student body approved the finance plan for the new football stadium. On April 2, 1970, about 250 students engaged in a <a href="/wiki/Sit-in" title="Sit-in">sit-in</a> in Montana Hall to protest Myers' termination, but it ended peacefully a day later. Myers was terminated, and another eight faculty resigned in protest. But during his year in office, the university completed Cobleigh Hall (ironically named for the last individual to be named acting president).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tough_fiscal_times_of_the_1970s">Tough fiscal times of the 1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Tough fiscal times of the 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bobcat_Stadium_detail_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1069" data-file-width="2500" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Bobcat_Stadium_detail_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/350px-Bobcat_Stadium_detail_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Bobcat_Stadium_detail_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/525px-Bobcat_Stadium_detail_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Bobcat_Stadium_detail_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/700px-Bobcat_Stadium_detail_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bobcat_Stadium_detail_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bobcat Stadium, shown here in 2013, opened in 1973 and was originally known as Reno H. Sales Stadium. It received its current name in 1998.</div></div></div>
<p>Dr. <a href="/wiki/Carl_W._McIntosh" title="Carl W. McIntosh">Carl W. McIntosh</a> was named MSU's eighth president in June 1970. Previously the president of 28,000-student <a href="/wiki/California_State_University,_Long_Beach" title="California State University, Long Beach">California State University, Long Beach</a>, McIntosh brought a consultative and deliberate style of decision-making to the university. He faced a poor fiscal climate: The state was entering a decade-long depression brought about by a steep drop in commodity prices, the state's higher education system had grown too large and unwieldy, and Governor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Lee_Judge" title="Thomas Lee Judge">Thomas L. Judge</a> had established a blue-ribbon committee to close several of the state's colleges. In 1974, women faculty at MSU sued, alleging <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">gender discrimination</a>. They won their suit in 1976, leading to a $400,000 damages award, a back-pay award, and extensive promotions (which also increased salaries). To accommodate these fiscal realities, McIntosh ordered several doctoral and master's degree programs terminated, and all advanced degree programs in the social sciences and liberal arts canceled.
</p><p>But McIntosh also scored a number of successes. In 1972, he persuaded the legislature to allow MSU to participate in the Washington, Wyoming, Alaska, Montana, and Idaho (WWAMI) medical education program, which allowed 20 (now 30) Montana citizens per year to begin medical school at MSU before completing studies at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">University of Washington</a>. The college of nursing (Sherrick Hall) was finished in 1973, and after three long years of construction Reno H. Sales Stadium (now Bobcat Stadium and Martel Field) and the Marga Hosaeus Fitness Center both opened. In 1974, the long-planned Creative Arts Complex (Cheever Hall, Haynes Hall, and Howard Hall) was also completed. Unfortunately, major increases in inflation led to significant design changes. Instead of a 1,200-seat concert hall with superb acoustics, a cramped and aurally dead 260-seat auditorium was built. Finally, in 1976, the university completed the new medical science building, Leon Johnson Hall.
</p><p>In 1976, the "hidden million" controversy ended McIntosh's tenure as president. In 1975, Montana's first Commissioner of Higher Education, Dr. Lawrence K. Pettit (a former MSU professor of political science) launched an investigation of several Montana colleges and universities. He was particularly interested in MSU, where McIntosh's laid-back governance style was widely considered to have hurt the university. In March 1976, Pettit announced he was confiscating $1 million in surplus student fees from MSU — money he argued the university was trying to hide from state auditors and the legislature. In fact, the monies were the result of excessively high enrollment in the 1974-1975 school year, and were intended to help see the university through the 1975-1976 school year (when the legislature would not meet, and thus could not provide the needed budgetary boost to handle the over-enrollment).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Pettit all but accused MSU and McIntosh of fraud, and McIntosh refused to attack Pettit's statements as mischaracterizations and slander. The public outcry about the "hidden million" led the Board of Regents to request McIntosh's resignation on June 30, 1977, which he tendered. (Pettit resigned the following year, his combative attempt to turn the commissioner's office into a sort of chancellorship having failed.)
</p><p>Located on the south side of <a href="/wiki/Bozeman,_Montana" title="Bozeman, Montana">Bozeman</a>, the university's 1,170 acres (4,700,000 m<sup>2</sup>) campus is the largest in the state. The elevation of the campus is 4,900 feet (1,500 m) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMSL" title="AMSL">above sea level</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resurgence_and_retrenchment_under_Tietz">Resurgence and retrenchment under Tietz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Resurgence and retrenchment under Tietz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dr. <a href="/wiki/William_Tietz" title="William Tietz">William Tietz</a>, MSU's ninth president, arrived in August 1977 just as economic conditions in the state were improving. With three of the four vice presidencies at the university open, Tietz imposed his stamp on the administration almost immediately. This included a strong emphasis on research, faculty development, better teaching, and diversity (particularly for Native Americans, the handicapped, and women). His aggressiveness, energy, and immediate rebudgeting of funds into faculty sabbaticals helped win over professors, who voted against <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">unionization</a> in 1978. Tietz's major goal, increasing research funding, was greatly helped by a 1981 decision of the legislature to refund indirect cost payments back to the university. This led to an immediate 15 percent recovery of in federal funds, and in time private foundation funding rose significantly as well.
</p><p>Only two buildings were constructed during Tietz's presidency — the Visual Communications Building in 1983 and the Plant Growth Center in 1987. Most of his focus as president was on raising salaries. A third building, the modern home of the Museum of the Rockies, opened in 1989. But this structure was paid for by bonds. Faculty salaries had declined 23 percent during the 1970s (due to wage freezes) and MSU was in the bottom 10 percent of salaries for faculty nationwide. <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_State_Research,_Education,_and_Extension_Service#Cooperative_Extension_System" title="Cooperative State Research, Education, and Extension Service">Cooperative Extension Service</a> salaries were dead last in the nation. The state legislature implemented a new salary funding formula that rectified many of these problems. Some university programs were also reestablished, such as the honors program, and some new ones formed, such as the Writing Center.
</p><p>The state once more entered a severe economic downturn in the mid 1980s. Budget cuts totaling nearly 10 percent, coupled with an enrollment shortfall, led to significant retrenchment. Tietz argued MSU should focus on its strongest programs. Thus, a wide array of programs were terminated: Membership in the Center for Research Libraries; sports like skiing, women's gymnastics, and wrestling; degree programs like engineering science, business education, and industrial arts; and the office of institutional research. Departments were merged and downsized, and Tietz proposed closing the School of Architecture. A battle broke out to save it, and Tietz backed off his decision. Tietz increasingly blamed Governor <a href="/wiki/Ted_Schwinden" title="Ted Schwinden">Ted Schwinden</a> for a failure to support higher education, and lashed out repeatedly against the governor when Schwinden publicly ridiculed MSU's new Tech Park (a 90-acre (360,000 m<sup>2</sup>) project designed to function as a technology incubator). Although a second faculty unionization effort failed in 1989, Tietz resigned in March 1990, frustrated by the constant battles with an "old guard" resistant to turning MSU toward high technology.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Centennial_and_expansion">Centennial and expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Centennial and expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_NE_Engineering_and_Physical_Sciences_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1362" data-file-width="3000" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Looking_NE_Engineering_and_Physical_Sciences_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg/220px-Looking_NE_Engineering_and_Physical_Sciences_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Looking_NE_Engineering_and_Physical_Sciences_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg/330px-Looking_NE_Engineering_and_Physical_Sciences_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Looking_NE_Engineering_and_Physical_Sciences_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg/440px-Looking_NE_Engineering_and_Physical_Sciences_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Looking_NE_Engineering_and_Physical_Sciences_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Engineering and Physical Sciences Building, completed in 1999.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michael_P._Malone" title="Michael P. Malone">Michael P. Malone</a> was named MSU's Acting President on January 1, 1991,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Native_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Native-20">[20]</a></sup>  and permanently appointed to the position in March 1991, Malone was named MSU's 10th president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloneDies_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaloneDies-21">[21]</a></sup> He had served as MSU's Dean of Graduate Studies from 1979 to 1988,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewistonobit_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewistonobit-22">[22]</a></sup> and then three one-year temporary appointments as Vice President for Academic Affairs while a fruitless nation search occurred for a permanent replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Native_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Native-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewistonobit_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewistonobit-22">[22]</a></sup> As Dean of Graduate Studies, he'd been critical of what he perceived as the state's unwillingnes to invest in high technology education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Malone's governance style was democratic, friendly, and personal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloneDies_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-MaloneDies-21">[21]</a></sup> His friendly style made him personally popular with legislators and earned their respect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloneDies_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-MaloneDies-21">[21]</a></sup> Nonetheless, he was criticized for focusing too much about how little money MSU had and for criticizing the legislature too much for not investing in higher education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchontzlerHardAct_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchontzlerHardAct-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Malone was the first MSU president to preside over the Billings, Great Falls, and Havre campuses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewistonobit_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lewistonobit-22">[22]</a></sup> On July 1, 1994, Montana restructured the Montana University System. Eastern Montana College in Billings, Montana Northern College in Havre, and the Vocational-Technical Center in Great Falls lost their independence and were made satellite campuses of Montana State University. Although Montana's seven tribal colleges remained independent (as they are sponsored by sovereign nations), the state required them to integrate their teaching, operations, and academic operations with both Montana State University and the University of Montana in order to continue to receive state funding.
</p><p>Montana State University celebrated its centennial in 1993.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aasheim_Gate_-_Malone_Centennial_Mall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1523" data-file-width="2300" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Aasheim_Gate_-_Malone_Centennial_Mall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/220px-Aasheim_Gate_-_Malone_Centennial_Mall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Aasheim_Gate_-_Malone_Centennial_Mall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/330px-Aasheim_Gate_-_Malone_Centennial_Mall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Aasheim_Gate_-_Malone_Centennial_Mall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg/440px-Aasheim_Gate_-_Malone_Centennial_Mall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman%2C_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aasheim_Gate_-_Malone_Centennial_Mall_-_Montana_State_University_-_Bozeman,_Montana_-_2013-07-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The western entrance to Centennial Mall, constructed to celebrate MSU's 100th anniversary in 1993.</div></div></div>
<p>During Malone's presidency, Montana State University witnessed "one of the greatest expansions in campus history", as a large number of new buildings were constructed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloneDies_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-MaloneDies-21">[21]</a></sup> These included the $1 million Centennial Mall (1993), the $22 million Engineering and Physical Sciences Building (1997), the $10 million Bobcat Stadium renovation, the $13.5 million renovation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worthington_Arena" title="Worthington Arena">Brick Breeden Fieldhouse</a>, the $12 million Agricultural Biosciences Building (1999), and the $7.5 million Renne Library renovation (1999).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloneDies_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-MaloneDies-21">[21]</a></sup> A strong sports fan, Malone's focus extended to sports personnel as well as sports facilities. In 1999, he fired <a href="/wiki/Montana_State_Bobcats_football" title="Montana State Bobcats football">Bobcats football</a> head coach Cliff Hysell after eight losing seasons and hired <a href="/wiki/Mike_Kramer" title="Mike Kramer">Mike Kramer</a>, the winning coach at <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Washington_University" title="Eastern Washington University">Eastern Washington University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In October 1999, he fired MSU women's basketball head coach Tracey Sheehan and assistant coach Jeff Malby after an NCAA investigation revealed that the two coaches were overworking their team and causing injuries to atudent-athletes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSUWomen_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSUWomen-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like William Tietz before him, Malone also pushed hard for faculty and the university to seek and win federal funding for scientific research. Federal research funding grew from just $13 million in the late 1980s to more than $50 million in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BDCObit_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-BDCObit-27">[27]</a></sup> The undergraduate curriculum was revamped,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BDCObit_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-BDCObit-27">[27]</a></sup> enrollment hit a historic high of 11,746 students in 1999,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloneDies_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-MaloneDies-21">[21]</a></sup> and the Burns Telecommunications Center was established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BDCObit_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-BDCObit-27">[27]</a></sup> Malone benefitted from a strong economy that eased many of the fiscal pressures Tietz faced. He expanded alumni fund-raising programs, and pushed the MSU Foundation to redouble its fund-raising efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BDCObit_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-BDCObit-27">[27]</a></sup> But the legislature was not forthcoming with salary increases. He weathered a <a href="/wiki/Strike_action" title="Strike action">strike</a> by clerical and administrative support staff in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloneDies_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-MaloneDies-21">[21]</a></sup> He was later criticized, however, for initiating projects without having the money to complete them and then using the subsequent construction crisis to raise the funds to finish the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchontzlerHardAct_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-SchontzlerHardAct-24">[24]</a></sup> Tuition doubled during his time in office, angering students, and some faculty criticized his willingness to construct new buildings while declining to pay for teaching equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloneDies_21-7"><a href="#cite_note-MaloneDies-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The MSU community was shocked when Malone died of a heart attack at 1:15 A.M. at <a href="/wiki/Bozeman_Yellowstone_International_Airport" title="Bozeman Yellowstone International Airport">Bozeman Yellowstone International Airport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloneDies_21-8"><a href="#cite_note-MaloneDies-21">[21]</a></sup> He was the second MSU president to die in office, and the second to die of heart failure.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Twenty-first_century_stability">Twenty-first century stability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Twenty-first century stability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_NW_at_Chemistry_and_Biochemistry_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1786" data-file-width="2500" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Looking_NW_at_Chemistry_and_Biochemistry_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg/220px-Looking_NW_at_Chemistry_and_Biochemistry_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Looking_NW_at_Chemistry_and_Biochemistry_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg/330px-Looking_NW_at_Chemistry_and_Biochemistry_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Looking_NW_at_Chemistry_and_Biochemistry_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg/440px-Looking_NW_at_Chemistry_and_Biochemistry_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Looking_NW_at_Chemistry_and_Biochemistry_Building_-_Montana_State_University_-_2013-07-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Chemistry and Biochemistry Building.</div></div></div>
<p>Malone's successor, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Gamble" title="Geoffrey Gamble">Geoffrey Gamble</a>, was named the 11th president of Montana State University on October 5, 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> His governance style was open and consultative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellig_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellig-29">[29]</a></sup> In addition to making the president's executive council more representative<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchontzlerHardAct_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-SchontzlerHardAct-24">[24]</a></sup> and reaching out to the Faculty Senate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellig_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ellig-29">[29]</a></sup> he established a new 25-member University Planning, Budget and Analysis Committee to establish the university budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchontzlerHardAct_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-SchontzlerHardAct-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellig_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ellig-29">[29]</a></sup> Legislatively, Gamble promoted MSU's accomplishments, praised legislators for their financial support (even when it was not forthcoming), and spoke of state funding for the university in terms of investment that led to economic and job growth. According to Cathy Conover, MSU's chief legislative lobbyist, Gamble's style was "a sea change" that led the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>-dominated state legislature to rave about him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchontzlerHardAct_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-SchontzlerHardAct-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Montana State University also implemented the "Core 2.0 curriculum" during Gamble's tenure as president. This program encourages undergraduate students to engage in research or practice their art prior to graduation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellig_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ellig-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gamble also focused on research. Between 2000 and 2009, federal research funding at MSU grew by 61 percent to $98.4 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RecordEnroll_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-RecordEnroll-30">[30]</a></sup> Gamble <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademarked</a> the name "University of the Yellowstone" to reflect the high level of research MSU conducted in the greater <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a> ecosystem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gamble also made diversity a major effort of his presidency. He appointed the university's first permanent female vice president, and by 2009 women outnumbered men among MSU's deans, five to four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchontzlerHardAct_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-SchontzlerHardAct-24">[24]</a></sup> He appointed Dr. Henrietta Mann (chair of the MSU Department of Native American Studies, and one of the most prominent Indian educators in the United States) his personal representative to the seven tribal colleges which participate in the <a href="/wiki/Montana_University_System" title="Montana University System">Montana University System</a> and created a Council of Elders to bring leaders of the tribal colleges together twice a year at MSU for discussions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellig_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ellig-29">[29]</a></sup> Native American enrollment at MSU rose 79 percent (to a historic high of 377 students) during Gamble's time in office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RecordEnroll_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-RecordEnroll-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, a major sports scandal engulfed Montana State University. On June 30, 2006, former MSU basketball player Branden Miller and former MSU football player John LeBrum were charged with murdering local <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> dealer Jason Wright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-34">[34]</a></sup> After an 18-month investigation, six additional current and former MSU athletes were charged with buying and selling cocaine. Three of the six were charged with running a cocaine smuggling ring that sold 26 pounds (12 kg) of cocaine in Bozeman between June 2005 to May 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Court records later revealed that some MSU coaches knew Miller carried handguns in his athletic bag at school and that the murder weapon and other handguns had been secreted in Brick Breeden Fieldhouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In August 2007, <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i> ran a front-page article, "Trouble in Paradise", that recounted drug use, violence, theft, intimidation, and illegal activities by current and former MSU student athletes and the complicity of low-level coaching staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> An investigation by the NCAA revealed significantly lower graduation rates for MSU football and basketball players under football coach Mike Kramer as well as men's basketball coach Mick Durham, and a large number of athletes on or flirting with <a href="/wiki/Scholastic_probation" title="Scholastic probation">academic probation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchontzlerHardAct_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-SchontzlerHardAct-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HotWater_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-HotWater-37">[37]</a></sup> Gamble quickly fired Kramer, who then sued MSU for unlawful dismissal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchontzlerHardAct_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-SchontzlerHardAct-24">[24]</a></sup> Kramer and MSU settled out of court, and Kramer received a payment of $240,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HotWater_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-HotWater-37">[37]</a></sup> In 2009, Gamble said his hardest time as president was dealing with the sports scandal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retire_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Retire-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gamble announced his retirement on March 22, 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retire_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Retire-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Record_growth:_2010-2014">Record growth: 2010-2014</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Record growth: 2010-2014">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Waded Cruzado succeeded Gamble as president, taking office on Jan. 4, 2010. Since her arrival, the university’s headcount enrollment has grown from 13,559 in the fall of 2010 to a record 16,902 in the fall of 2018 – a 24.66 percent increase – making MSU the largest university in the state of Montana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missoulian.com_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-missoulian.com-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to enrollment increases, the campus has seen the completion of numerous major construction and renovation projects since Cruzado’s arrival. In the fall of 2010, the university reopened one of its most heavily used classroom buildings on campus, Gaines Hall, after a $32 million renovation funded by the Montana Legislature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>That same fall, the university opened its new, 40,000-square-foot Animal Bioscience Building. The $15.7 million building was funded, in part, by donations from Montana’s livestock and grains industry. In addition to classroom and teaching laboratory space, the building is home to the MSU College of Agriculture’s Department of Animal and Range Sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the Gaines Hall renovation and the Animal Biosciences building were underway before Cruzado took office, in the fall of 2010 she launched an ambitious 90-day campaign to raise $6 million in private donations for a $10 million project to replace and expand the 38-year-old south end zone of the university’s football stadium. The university would cover the remaining $4 million for the project, paying it back from revenues generated by MSU Athletics, including ticket sales. The campaign was successful and resulted in a new end zone opening for the fall 2011 season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The end zone project resulted in a net gain of 5,200 seats for the stadium for a total capacity of 17,500. However, through additional standing-room-only attendance, the stadium thrice exceeded 21,000 spectators in the fall of 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The fall of 2010 also marked the official opening of Gallatin College Programs at MSU, which offers two-year education on campus. The two-year program had been formerly known as MSU-Great Falls College of Technology in Bozeman and was located away from the central campus. With the renaming, Gallatin College was also given offices and classrooms in Hamilton Hall, located in the campus center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>MSU marked its 125th anniversary in 2018 with a year of celebratory events, including the Bobcat Birthday Bash on campus. Several thousand attended daylong events on Feb. 16-17 featuring family activities, music, fireworks and speeches commemorating the university's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> A newly installed statue of Abraham Lincoln by Bozeman-area artist <a href="/wiki/Jim_Dolan_(sculptor)" title="Jim Dolan (sculptor)">Jim Dolan</a> was unveiled at a ceremony honoring the former president's contributions to <a href="/wiki/Land-grant_university" title="Land-grant university">land-grant universities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="infobox" style="width: 22em">
<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align: center; font-size: 125%"><a href="/wiki/College_and_university_rankings" title="College and university rankings">University rankings</a>

<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="background-color: lavender; text-align: center">National
</th></tr>

<th><i><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">ARWU</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rankings_ARWU_NU_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_ARWU_NU-46">[46]</a></sup>
</th>
<td>138-150
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rankings_USNWR_NU_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_USNWR_NU-47">[47]</a></sup>
</th>
<td>207
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>MSU is the national leader for <a href="/wiki/Phi_Kappa_Phi" title="Phi Kappa Phi">Phi Kappa Phi</a> Graduate Fellowships and is among the top ten institutions in the country for recipients of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goldwater_Scholarship" title="Goldwater Scholarship">Goldwater Scholarships</a>, having produced 70 of the scholars as of May 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The university counts among its graduates several recipients of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhodes_scholar" title="Rhodes scholar">Rhodes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Truman_scholarship" title="Truman scholarship">Truman</a> scholarships, and MSU has consistently produced winners of <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i> Academic <a href="/wiki/All-America" title="All-America">All-America</a> honors.  <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._News_and_World_Report" title="U.S. News and World Report">U.S. News and World Report</a></i> has routinely listed MSU as one of America's "best buys" for undergraduate education, and ranks it in the third tier of National Universities. Montana State University offers the world's only <a href="/wiki/MSU_MFA_Program_in_Science_%26_Natural_History_Filmmaking" title="MSU MFA Program in Science &amp; Natural History Filmmaking">Master of Fine Arts degree in Science and Natural History Filmmaking</a>, and MSU's Museum of the Rockies is home to the largest T. Rex skull ever found—bigger, even, than "Sue" at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicago_Field_Museum" title="Chicago Field Museum">Chicago Field Museum</a>.
</p><p>Montana State University refers to itself as "the University of the Yellowstone," for its extensive research and scholarly activities concerning the Greater Yellowstone Ecosystem. Montana State University has received more than five times the number of National Science Foundation grants for Yellowstone studies than its nearest competition, Stanford and UCLA, according to David Roberts, head of MSU's ecology department.
</p><p>Academic programs, procedures and policies are overseen by the Office of the Executive Vice President for Academic Affairs and Provost. This office handles all teaching-related issues and is responsible for faculty hiring, establishing academic programs and curricula, course scheduling and accreditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The position has been held since April 2017 by Robert Mokwa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> He succeeded Martha Potvin, who in 2010 became the university's first female provost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Montana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Montana State University maintains extensive research programs, providing opportunities for undergraduates, graduates, and advanced graduate students. The university is in the top 3 percent of colleges and universities in the United States in research expenditures<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and regularly reports annual research expenditures in excess of $100 million, including a record $130.8 million in the fiscal year that ended in June 2017. In that same year, the university said its faculty wrote 1,729 grant applications, which led to 526 new grant awards worth roughly $75 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>MSU's Office of Research and Economic Development coordinates programs that encourage faculty to pursue externally funded research. Its Office of Research Compliance oversees programs that promotes ethical and responsible research and ensures compliance with local, state, and federal regulations for research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The Office of Sponsored programs manages financial, reporting, compliance, auditing and related tasks for externally funded research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The university maintains a technology transfer office  to commercialize MSU faculty inventions, spur businesses based on those technologies and network with businesses looking to license MSU technologies. The office manages more than 500 technologies and 375 patents, trademarks and copyrights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
